--- Lesson 2.7: Human Impact on Ecosystems (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Human Impact on Ecosystems
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is deforestation?
Deforestation is the large-scale clearing or removal of forests, primarily for agriculture, logging, or urban development. It reduces biodiversity by destroying habitats, increases atmospheric CO₂ (since trees absorb carbon), contributes to soil erosion, and disrupts water cycles.
What is pollution in an environmental context?
Pollution is the introduction of harmful substances (chemicals, plastics, heavy metals) or energy (noise, light, heat) into the environment. It can contaminate air, water, and soil, harming organisms and disrupting ecosystem functions.
What is habitat fragmentation?
Habitat fragmentation occurs when human activities (roads, agriculture, urbanization) break large continuous habitats into smaller, isolated patches. This reduces habitat area, limits species movement between patches, increases edge effects, and makes small populations more vulnerable to extinction.
What is overexploitation?
Overexploitation occurs when humans harvest a resource (fish, wildlife, timber, water) faster than it can be naturally replenished. This leads to population declines, ecosystem disruption, and potentially species extinction. Examples include overfishing, overhunting, and excessive logging.
What is the greenhouse effect?
The greenhouse effect is a natural process where gases like CO₂, methane, and water vapor trap heat in Earth's atmosphere, keeping the planet warm enough for life. Human activities (burning fossil fuels, deforestation) enhance this effect by adding extra greenhouse gases, causing global warming.
Real-World Applications
Example: The Amazon rainforest loses approximately 10,000 km² of forest per year due to cattle ranching and soybean farming. Satellite images show that deforested areas have higher surface temperatures, less rainfall, and heavily eroded soil compared to adjacent forested areas. Which human impact does this scenario BEST illustrate?
This scenario illustrates deforestation — the large-scale clearing of tropical rainforest for cattle ranching and agriculture. The consequences (higher temperatures, less rainfall, erosion) are classic effects of deforestation: loss of the forest's cooling effect, disruption of the water cycle (trees transpire enormous quantities of water), and removal of root systems that held soil in place.
Example: The Great Pacific Garbage Patch is a massive accumulation of plastic debris in the North Pacific Ocean, covering an area twice the size of Texas. Marine organisms from tiny plankton to sea turtles and seabirds ingest plastic fragments, and toxic chemicals from plastics accumulate through food chains. Which concept does this scenario BEST illustrate?
This illustrates pollution — specifically plastic pollution and biomagnification. Plastic debris and the toxic chemicals it absorbs enter the marine food chain when small organisms ingest microplastics. These toxins then biomagnify (concentrate at higher levels) as predators consume contaminated prey, affecting organisms across all trophic levels.
Example: In the 1970s, acid rain from coal-burning power plants in the Midwest United States damaged forests and lakes hundreds of miles downwind in the northeastern states. Lakes became too acidic for fish and aquatic invertebrates, and trees suffered nutrient loss from acidified soil. After the Clean Air Act amendments reduced SO₂ emissions by 90%, lake acidity gradually decreased and some fish populations returned. What does this scenario illustrate about human impact and conservation?
This scenario illustrates how air pollution (SO₂ and NOₓ from burning fossil fuels) can travel hundreds of miles and damage distant ecosystems through acid rain. It also shows that effective conservation legislation (the Clean Air Act) can reverse some damage — reducing emissions allowed lake ecosystems to start recovering, demonstrating that human action can both harm and help ecosystems.

--- Lesson 2.7: Human Impact on Ecosystems (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What is deforestation?
    [ ] The growth of forests after a wildfire
    [ ] The planting of new trees in areas that were not previously forested
    [ ] The natural process of trees shedding their leaves in autumn
    [*] The large-scale removal of forests, which reduces biodiversity and increases atmospheric CO₂
    Explanation: Deforestation is the large-scale clearing or removal of forests, primarily for agriculture, logging, or urban development. It reduces biodiversity by destroying habitats, increases atmospheric CO₂ (since trees absorb carbon), contributes to soil erosion, and disrupts water cycles.

  Q2. What is pollution in an environmental context?
    [ ] The movement of water through the water cycle
    [ ] The natural recycling of nutrients through ecosystems
    [ ] The process of organisms decomposing dead matter
    [*] The introduction of harmful substances or energy into the environment that cause damage to ecosystems
    Explanation: Pollution is the introduction of harmful substances (chemicals, plastics, heavy metals) or energy (noise, light, heat) into the environment. It can contaminate air, water, and soil, harming organisms and disrupting ecosystem functions.

  Q3. What are invasive species?
    [ ] Endangered species that need protection
    [ ] Species that have always lived in an area
    [*] Non-native organisms that spread rapidly in new environments and harm native species and ecosystems
    [ ] Any species that migrates seasonally between habitats
    Explanation: Invasive species are non-native organisms introduced to a new environment where they spread rapidly and cause ecological harm. They often lack natural predators in their new habitat, allowing their populations to explode and outcompete, prey on, or bring disease to native species.

  Q4. What is habitat fragmentation?
    [ ] The natural process of habitats changing over geologic time
    [*] The division of large, continuous habitats into smaller, isolated patches by human activities like roads, farms, and cities
    [ ] The merging of two separate habitats into one large area
    [ ] The creation of new habitats by planting native species
    Explanation: Habitat fragmentation occurs when human activities (roads, agriculture, urbanization) break large continuous habitats into smaller, isolated patches. This reduces habitat area, limits species movement between patches, increases edge effects, and makes small populations more vulnerable to extinction.

  Q5. What does sustainability mean in an environmental context?
    [ ] Preserving the environment by stopping all human activity
    [ ] Using all natural resources as quickly as possible
    [ ] Maximizing economic growth regardless of environmental impact
    [*] Meeting present needs without compromising future generations' ability to meet their own needs
    Explanation: Sustainability means using resources at a rate that allows natural systems to replenish them, ensuring that future generations can also meet their needs. It balances economic development, social well-being, and environmental protection.

  Q6. What is overexploitation?
    [ ] The natural extinction of species over geologic time
    [ ] Protecting natural resources through conservation laws
    [*] Harvesting resources (fish, trees, wildlife) faster than they can naturally replenish
    [ ] Using renewable energy sources instead of fossil fuels
    Explanation: Overexploitation occurs when humans harvest a resource (fish, wildlife, timber, water) faster than it can be naturally replenished. This leads to population declines, ecosystem disruption, and potentially species extinction. Examples include overfishing, overhunting, and excessive logging.

  Q7. How does climate change affect ecosystems?
    [ ] It improves all ecosystems by making them warmer
    [*] It alters temperatures, precipitation, and sea levels, forcing species to adapt, migrate, or face extinction
    [ ] It affects only polar regions and nowhere else
    [ ] Climate change has no effect on ecosystems
    Explanation: Climate change affects ecosystems worldwide by raising temperatures, altering precipitation patterns, melting ice, raising sea levels, and increasing extreme weather events. Species must adapt, migrate to suitable habitats, or face extinction. It disrupts seasonal timing (phenology), coral reefs, polar habitats, and many other ecosystems.

  Q8. What is the greenhouse effect?
    [ ] A phenomenon that only occurs inside greenhouses used for growing plants
    [ ] A process that cools Earth's surface by reflecting sunlight
    [*] A natural process where atmospheric gases trap heat and warm Earth's surface, which is enhanced by human CO₂ emissions
    [ ] A phenomenon caused entirely by volcanic activity
    Explanation: The greenhouse effect is a natural process where gases like CO₂, methane, and water vapor trap heat in Earth's atmosphere, keeping the planet warm enough for life. Human activities (burning fossil fuels, deforestation) enhance this effect by adding extra greenhouse gases, causing global warming.

  Q9. What are the five main human impacts on ecosystems?
    [ ] Photosynthesis, respiration, decomposition, precipitation, and erosion
    [*] Habitat destruction, pollution, overexploitation, invasive species, and climate change
    [ ] Predation, competition, mutualism, parasitism, and commensalism
    [ ] Mitosis, meiosis, transcription, translation, and replication
    Explanation: The five major categories of human impact on ecosystems are: (1) habitat destruction — clearing forests, draining wetlands, urbanization; (2) pollution — contaminating air, water, and soil; (3) overexploitation — overfishing, overhunting, overharvesting; (4) invasive species — introducing non-native species; and (5) climate change — global warming from greenhouse gas emissions.

  Q10. Name two positive actions humans can take to protect ecosystems.
    [ ] Increasing deforestation and introducing more invasive species
    [ ] Burning more fossil fuels and dumping waste in oceans
    [*] Establishing protected areas (national parks, marine sanctuaries) and using sustainable practices
    [ ] Eliminating all predators and draining all wetlands
    Explanation: Positive human actions include creating protected areas (national parks, wildlife refuges, marine sanctuaries), using sustainable practices (renewable energy, recycling, sustainable agriculture), enforcing environmental regulations, restoring degraded habitats, and reducing greenhouse gas emissions.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes deforestation?
    [ ] The planting of new trees to replace those cut down
    [ ] The process of forests regrowing after a natural disturbance
    [ ] The natural loss of leaves during autumn in deciduous forests
    [*] The large-scale clearing, burning, or removal of forests, converting forested land to non-forest uses
    Explanation: Deforestation is the large-scale clearing, burning, or removal of forests to convert the land to agriculture, urban development, or other non-forest uses. It leads to habitat loss, biodiversity decline, increased CO₂ in the atmosphere, soil erosion, and disrupted water cycles.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes pollution?
    [ ] The natural decomposition of organic matter in soil
    [ ] The release of oxygen by plants during photosynthesis
    [ ] The cycling of nutrients through an ecosystem
    [*] The introduction of harmful substances or contaminants into the natural environment, causing adverse changes
    Explanation: Pollution is the introduction of harmful substances or contaminants (chemicals, plastics, heavy metals, excess nutrients, noise, light) into the natural environment, causing adverse changes to ecosystems and organism health. Major types include air, water, and soil pollution.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes an invasive species?
    [*] A non-native organism introduced to a new environment where it outcompetes native species and disrupts the ecosystem
    [ ] Any species that migrates between habitats during seasonal changes
    [ ] An endangered species that requires protection from hunters
    [ ] A native species that has become dominant in its own ecosystem
    Explanation: An invasive species is a non-native organism that has been introduced (intentionally or accidentally) to a new environment where it lacks natural predators or competitors. It reproduces rapidly, outcompetes native species for resources, and disrupts the ecosystem's balance. Examples include kudzu, zebra mussels, and cane toads.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes habitat fragmentation?
    [ ] The creation of wildlife corridors to connect habitats
    [ ] The natural division of ecosystems by rivers and mountains
    [*] The breaking up of large, continuous habitats into smaller, isolated patches by human activities like road building and development
    [ ] The complete destruction and removal of all habitat in an area
    Explanation: Habitat fragmentation is the process by which large, continuous habitats are broken into smaller, isolated patches by human activities such as road building, urban development, and agriculture. It reduces biodiversity, isolates populations (reducing gene flow), creates edge effects, and makes species more vulnerable to extinction.

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes sustainability?
    [ ] Using as many resources as possible before they run out
    [ ] Returning the environment to its state before human civilization
    [*] Meeting present human needs while preserving natural resources and ecosystems so future generations can also meet their needs
    [ ] Keeping ecosystems completely untouched by any human activity
    Explanation: Sustainability is the practice of meeting present human needs while preserving natural resources, ecosystems, and environmental quality so that future generations can also meet their needs. It balances economic development, social well-being, and environmental protection.

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes overexploitation?
    [*] Harvesting a natural resource (fish, wildlife, timber) at a rate faster than the population or supply can replenish itself
    [ ] Using renewable energy sources to power cities
    [ ] Protecting endangered species through breeding programs
    [ ] Studying the population dynamics of wild species
    Explanation: Overexploitation is the harvesting of a natural resource (fish, wildlife, timber, freshwater) at a rate faster than the population or supply can naturally replenish itself. This leads to severe population declines, ecosystem disruption, and potentially species extinction (e.g., Atlantic cod collapse, passenger pigeon extinction).

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes climate change?
    [ ] The daily variation in temperature and precipitation at a location
    [ ] A temporary change in seasons caused by Earth's tilt
    [*] A long-term shift in global temperatures and weather patterns, largely driven by human greenhouse gas emissions since the Industrial Revolution
    [ ] The difference in climate between tropical and polar regions
    Explanation: Climate change refers to long-term shifts in global temperatures and weather patterns. While natural factors contribute, current climate change is largely driven by human greenhouse gas emissions (CO₂, methane) from burning fossil fuels, deforestation, and agriculture since the Industrial Revolution, causing global warming, sea level rise, and extreme weather.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes the greenhouse effect?
    [ ] The depletion of the ozone layer by chemical pollutants
    [ ] The warming of water in oceans due to volcanic activity
    [*] The natural warming process in which atmospheric gases (CO₂, methane, water vapor) trap heat radiated from Earth's surface
    [ ] The cooling of Earth's surface by cloud cover reflecting sunlight
    Explanation: The greenhouse effect is the natural warming process in which atmospheric gases (CO₂, methane, water vapor, nitrous oxide) trap heat radiated from Earth's surface, keeping the planet warm enough for life. Human activities enhance this effect by adding extra greenhouse gases, leading to global warming.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes biodiversity loss?
    [ ] The seasonal migration of animals to new habitats
    [ ] The natural process of evolution creating new species over time
    [ ] An increase in the number of individuals within a single species
    [*] The decline in the variety of life forms (species, genetic diversity, ecosystems) in a region or globally, often caused by human activities
    Explanation: Biodiversity loss is the decline in the variety of life forms — species diversity, genetic diversity, and ecosystem diversity — in a region or globally. It is primarily caused by human activities including habitat destruction, pollution, overexploitation, invasive species, and climate change. Loss of biodiversity weakens ecosystem stability and resilience.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes conservation?
    [ ] The study of fossils and ancient ecosystems
    [*] The planned management and protection of natural resources and ecosystems to prevent overuse, destruction, or neglect
    [ ] The process of urbanizing natural landscapes for human use
    [ ] The complete elimination of human interaction with nature
    Explanation: Conservation is the planned management and protection of natural resources, wildlife, and ecosystems to prevent overuse, destruction, or neglect. Conservation strategies include establishing protected areas (national parks, marine sanctuaries), captive breeding programs, habitat restoration, sustainable resource use, and environmental legislation.

  Q21. The Amazon rainforest loses approximately 10,000 km² of forest per year due to cattle ranching and soybean farming. Satellite images show that deforested areas have higher surface temperatures, less rainfall, and heavily eroded soil compared to adjacent forested areas. Which human impact does this scenario BEST illustrate?
    [ ] Overexploitation — the soil is being harvested faster than it can regenerate
    [ ] Invasive species — soybean plants are invasive organisms harming the rainforest
    [*] Deforestation — clearing forests for agriculture destroys habitat, increases CO₂, disrupts water cycles, and causes erosion
    [ ] Pollution — the cattle and soy farms release chemical pollutants into the air
    Explanation: This scenario illustrates deforestation — the large-scale clearing of tropical rainforest for cattle ranching and agriculture. The consequences (higher temperatures, less rainfall, erosion) are classic effects of deforestation: loss of the forest's cooling effect, disruption of the water cycle (trees transpire enormous quantities of water), and removal of root systems that held soil in place.

  Q22. The Great Pacific Garbage Patch is a massive accumulation of plastic debris in the North Pacific Ocean, covering an area twice the size of Texas. Marine organisms from tiny plankton to sea turtles and seabirds ingest plastic fragments, and toxic chemicals from plastics accumulate through food chains. Which concept does this scenario BEST illustrate?
    [ ] Overexploitation — humans are harvesting too much plastic from the ocean
    [ ] Habitat fragmentation — the garbage patch divides the ocean into isolated patches
    [ ] Invasive species — the plastics are non-native organisms disrupting the ecosystem
    [*] Pollution and biomagnification — plastic contaminants enter the food chain and concentrate at higher trophic levels
    Explanation: This illustrates pollution — specifically plastic pollution and biomagnification. Plastic debris and the toxic chemicals it absorbs enter the marine food chain when small organisms ingest microplastics. These toxins then biomagnify (concentrate at higher levels) as predators consume contaminated prey, affecting organisms across all trophic levels.

  Q23. In 1935, cane toads were introduced to Australia to control sugarcane beetles. The toads failed to control the beetles but spread rapidly across the continent because they had no natural predators. Their toxic skin kills native predators (quolls, snakes, crocodiles) that try to eat them, and they outcompete native frogs for food. What human impact does this BEST illustrate?
    [ ] Overexploitation — Australians overharvested the sugarcane beetles
    [ ] Pollution — the toads are releasing toxic chemicals that pollute the soil
    [*] Invasive species — an introduced organism with no natural predators outcompetes natives and disrupts the ecosystem
    [ ] Climate change — warming temperatures allowed the toads to spread
    Explanation: This is a classic example of the invasive species problem. Cane toads were intentionally introduced to control pests but became invasive themselves — they lacked natural predators in Australia, reproduced rapidly, poisoned native predators with their toxic skin, and outcompeted native amphibians for resources. This illustrates how introducing non-native species can have devastating unintended consequences.

  Q24. A new highway cuts through a large national forest, dividing it into two smaller sections. Researchers discover that deer populations on each side show reduced genetic diversity over 20 years, and small mammals like flying squirrels cannot cross the road to reach mates or food sources on the other side. Which concept does this BEST illustrate?
    [ ] Overexploitation — too many deer were hunted from the forest
    [ ] Pollution — vehicle emissions on the highway poison the animals
    [*] Habitat fragmentation — the road splits the habitat into isolated patches, reducing gene flow and limiting species movement
    [ ] Deforestation — the entire forest was cleared for development
    Explanation: This illustrates habitat fragmentation — the road divides a continuous habitat into smaller, isolated patches. The consequences shown (reduced genetic diversity, inability to cross for mates and food) are classic effects of fragmentation: isolated populations lose genetic diversity through inbreeding, and species that cannot cross barriers face reduced access to resources and mates.

  Q25. The Atlantic cod fishery off Newfoundland, Canada, collapsed in 1992 after decades of industrial-scale fishing. The cod population dropped by 99%, and even after a fishing moratorium, the population has not recovered 30 years later because the ecosystem shifted — species that cod once ate (shrimp, crab) now dominate and may prey on young cod. What does this BEST illustrate?
    [ ] Invasive species — shrimp and crab were introduced and outcompeted cod
    [*] Overexploitation — harvesting cod faster than they could reproduce caused a population collapse and permanent ecosystem shift
    [ ] Climate change — warming waters killed the cod population
    [ ] Pollution — industrial waste contaminated the cod's habitat
    Explanation: This is a textbook example of overexploitation — industrial fishing harvested Atlantic cod far faster than the population could reproduce, causing a 99% decline. The collapse also illustrates how overexploitation can trigger an irreversible ecosystem shift (regime shift): once cod were removed, their former prey (shrimp, crab) dominated and now prevent cod recovery by preying on juvenile cod.

  Q26. In the 1970s, acid rain from coal-burning power plants in the Midwest United States damaged forests and lakes hundreds of miles downwind in the northeastern states. Lakes became too acidic for fish and aquatic invertebrates, and trees suffered nutrient loss from acidified soil. After the Clean Air Act amendments reduced SO₂ emissions by 90%, lake acidity gradually decreased and some fish populations returned. What does this scenario illustrate about human impact and conservation?
    [ ] Invasive fish species replaced native ones after the lakes became acidic
    [*] Pollution can damage ecosystems far from its source, but effective legislation and emission reduction can allow ecosystems to begin recovering
    [ ] Climate change caused the acid rain, and the Clean Air Act stopped global warming
    [ ] Deforestation in the Midwest caused the lakes to become acidic
    Explanation: This scenario illustrates how air pollution (SO₂ and NOₓ from burning fossil fuels) can travel hundreds of miles and damage distant ecosystems through acid rain. It also shows that effective conservation legislation (the Clean Air Act) can reverse some damage — reducing emissions allowed lake ecosystems to start recovering, demonstrating that human action can both harm and help ecosystems.

  Q27. Ocean temperatures have risen, causing coral reefs worldwide to experience bleaching events. When water temperatures increase by just 1-2°C above normal, corals expel the symbiotic algae (zooxanthellae) that provide them with food and color. Without these algae, corals turn white and may die. The Great Barrier Reef has lost over 50% of its coral cover since 1995. Which human impact is the PRIMARY driver of this phenomenon?
    [ ] Invasive species — non-native algae are replacing zooxanthellae
    [*] Climate change — global warming raises ocean temperatures, triggering coral bleaching by disrupting the coral-algae symbiosis
    [ ] Overexploitation — too many corals are being harvested from reefs
    [ ] Deforestation — clearing coastal forests causes corals to bleach
    Explanation: Coral bleaching is primarily driven by climate change. As human greenhouse gas emissions warm the oceans, even a 1-2°C increase above normal causes corals to expel their symbiotic zooxanthellae algae. Without these algae, corals lose their food source and color (bleaching) and may die. The Great Barrier Reef's 50%+ decline demonstrates the devastating impact of climate change on marine ecosystems.

  Q28. In the 1960s, Rachel Carson's book 'Silent Spring' documented how the pesticide DDT accumulated through food chains — from sprayed crops to insects to songbirds to raptors. Bald eagles and peregrine falcons nearly went extinct because DDT caused their eggshells to thin and break. After DDT was banned in 1972, these raptor populations slowly recovered. What TWO concepts does this scenario BEST illustrate?
    [*] Pollution (biomagnification of DDT through food chains) and successful conservation (banning DDT allowed population recovery)
    [ ] Deforestation (trees were cut to spray DDT) and sustainability (DDT was a sustainable pesticide)
    [ ] Invasive species (DDT as an invasive chemical) and overexploitation (overharvesting eagles)
    [ ] Habitat fragmentation (DDT broke habitats apart) and climate change (warming caused thin eggshells)
    Explanation: This scenario illustrates two key concepts: (1) pollution and biomagnification — DDT accumulated through food chains, concentrating in top predators and causing reproductive failure; and (2) successful conservation — banning DDT through legislation (responding to Carson's scientific evidence) allowed eagle and falcon populations to recover, showing that environmental policy can reverse human damage.

  Q29. After wolves were reintroduced to Yellowstone National Park in 1995, the park's entire ecosystem transformed. Wolves reduced elk overgrazing, which allowed willows and aspens to regrow along streams. This attracted beavers, whose dams created new wetland habitats for fish, amphibians, and songbirds. Even river channels stabilized because tree roots held the banks in place. What does this scenario illustrate about conservation and ecosystem restoration?
    [*] Restoring a keystone species can trigger a trophic cascade that revitalizes an entire ecosystem, demonstrating the value of active conservation
    [ ] Wolves are an invasive species that disrupted Yellowstone's natural balance
    [ ] The elk population increased because wolves drove them to better habitats
    [ ] The beavers were the most important species in the ecosystem, not the wolves
    Explanation: The Yellowstone wolf reintroduction is one of ecology's great success stories. Restoring the keystone predator (wolves) triggered a trophic cascade: wolves → fewer elk → vegetation recovery → beaver return → wetland creation → increased biodiversity and even river channel stabilization. This demonstrates that active conservation (species reintroduction) can restore ecosystem function and that removing or restoring one keystone species can transform entire ecosystems.

  Q30. A coastal city implements a comprehensive sustainability plan: solar panels and wind turbines replace 60% of coal power, a marine protected area bans fishing in a degraded bay, green roofs and urban parks are built on old parking lots, and recycling reduces landfill waste by 40%. Five years later, air quality improves, fish populations rebound in the bay, urban wildlife increases, and carbon emissions drop. Which principle of sustainability does this BEST demonstrate?
    [ ] Only one strategy (renewable energy) is needed to achieve sustainability
    [*] Sustainability requires integrating multiple strategies — renewable energy, protected areas, habitat restoration, and waste reduction — to meet human needs while preserving ecosystems
    [ ] The improvements were due to natural ecological succession, not human action
    [ ] Sustainability means completely stopping all human development and industry
    Explanation: This scenario demonstrates the holistic nature of sustainability — meeting present human needs while preserving ecosystems for future generations. It requires integrating multiple strategies: renewable energy (replacing fossil fuels), conservation (marine protected areas), habitat restoration (green roofs, urban parks), and waste reduction (recycling). The positive results (cleaner air, recovered fish, urban wildlife, lower emissions) show that comprehensive sustainability planning can simultaneously benefit both human communities and natural ecosystems.

--- Lesson 3.1: Community Ecology (Summary) ---

  Key Concepts: Community Ecology
Overview
This lesson covers the fundamental measures of center used to analyze and summarize data sets. You'll learn how to calculate and apply the mean, median, and mode, and understand when each is most appropriate.
Key Definitions
What is a biological community?
A biological community consists of all the populations of different species living and interacting in a particular area at the same time. Unlike a population (one species) or an ecosystem (biotic + abiotic), a community focuses specifically on the interacting species. Community ecology studies how these species interact through competition, predation, symbiosis, and other relationships.
What is species richness?
Species richness is simply the count of different species present in a community. A tropical rainforest with 500 tree species has higher species richness than a temperate forest with 50 tree species. Species richness is one component of species diversity but does not account for how evenly individuals are distributed among those species.
What is a keystone species?
A keystone species exerts an influence on its community that is disproportionately large relative to its abundance or biomass. Removing a keystone species causes dramatic changes in community structure. The classic example is the sea star Pisaster in Pacific tide pools — its removal allows mussels to dominate, reducing species diversity from 15+ species to just a few.
What is a foundation species?
A foundation species creates or significantly modifies the physical habitat, enabling many other species to exist in the community. Unlike keystone species (influence disproportionate to abundance), foundation species typically have high abundance and physically structure the environment. Examples include coral (building reef structure), kelp (creating underwater forests), and large trees (forming forest canopy).
What is an indicator species?
An indicator species is one whose presence, absence, or health reflects specific environmental conditions in the community. Scientists monitor indicator species to assess ecosystem health. For example, amphibians are indicators of water quality (they absorb pollutants through their skin), and lichens indicate air quality.
What is the intermediate disturbance hypothesis?
The intermediate disturbance hypothesis proposes that species diversity is highest at moderate (intermediate) levels of disturbance. Too little disturbance allows competitive dominants to exclude other species; too much disturbance eliminates all but the hardiest colonizers. Moderate disturbance prevents any one species from dominating while not being so frequent that sensitive species cannot establish.
What is the difference between a dominant species and a keystone species?
A dominant species controls the community through its sheer abundance or biomass (e.g., sugar maples dominating a forest canopy). A keystone species has an effect disproportionate to its relatively low abundance (e.g., sea stars controlling mussel populations). Both influence community structure, but through different mechanisms — abundance vs. outsized ecological role.
What is bottom-up control in a community?
Bottom-up control (also called resource-driven control) means community structure is determined by factors at the base of the food web — nutrient availability, sunlight, and producer abundance. More nutrients → more producers → more herbivores → more predators. Changes ripple upward through trophic levels from the bottom.
What is top-down control in a community?
Top-down control (predator-driven control) means that predators at higher trophic levels regulate the abundance of organisms at lower levels. This can create a trophic cascade: removing a top predator causes herbivore populations to explode, which overgrazes producers. The Yellowstone wolf reintroduction is a classic example of restoring top-down control.
Outliers and Resistant Measures
This is a textbook example of the intermediate disturbance hypothesis. No fire (low disturbance) → competitive dominants exclude others. Annual fire (high disturbance) → only fire-resistant species survive. Fire every 3-5 years (moderate disturbance) → prevents competitive exclusion but is infrequent enough for fire-sensitive species to establish between burns, maximizing diversity.
Important Concepts
A study of grassland communities found that fire frequency determines species diversity. Plots that never burned became dominated by a few tall grass species. Plots burned every year supported only fire-resistant species. But plots burned every 3-5 years had the highest species diversity — both fire-tolerant and fire-sensitive species coexisted. Which concept does this BEST support
This is a textbook example of the intermediate disturbance hypothesis. No fire (low disturbance) → competitive dominants exclude others. Annual fire (high disturbance) → only fire-resistant species survive. Fire every 3-5 years (moderate disturbance) → prevents competitive exclusion but is infrequent enough for fire-sensitive species to establish between burns, maximizing diversity.

--- Lesson 3.1: Community Ecology (Quiz) ---

  Q1. What is a biological community?
    [*] All the populations of different species living and interacting in a particular area
    [ ] All the living and non-living components of an area combined
    [ ] The physical environment where organisms live
    [ ] All the individuals of one species in an area
    Explanation: A biological community consists of all the populations of different species living and interacting in a particular area at the same time. Unlike a population (one species) or an ecosystem (biotic + abiotic), a community focuses specifically on the interacting species. Community ecology studies how these species interact through competition, predation, symbiosis, and other relationships.

  Q2. What is species richness?
    [ ] The total number of individual organisms in a community
    [ ] How evenly distributed individuals are among species
    [*] The total number of different species present in a community
    [ ] The total biomass of all organisms in a community
    Explanation: Species richness is simply the count of different species present in a community. A tropical rainforest with 500 tree species has higher species richness than a temperate forest with 50 tree species. Species richness is one component of species diversity but does not account for how evenly individuals are distributed among those species.

  Q3. How does species diversity differ from species richness?
    [ ] Species diversity only measures the rarest species in a community
    [ ] Species diversity counts individual organisms while richness counts species
    [*] Species diversity combines both the number of species (richness) and the relative abundance of each species (evenness)
    [ ] Species diversity and species richness mean exactly the same thing
    Explanation: Species diversity is a more comprehensive measure than species richness because it combines two components: (1) species richness — the number of different species, and (2) species evenness — how equally individuals are distributed among those species. A community with 10 species equally represented has higher diversity than one with 10 species where 90% belong to one species.

  Q4. What is a keystone species?
    [ ] Any species at the top of the food chain
    [ ] A species that creates physical habitat structure for others
    [*] A species that has a disproportionately large effect on its community relative to its abundance
    [ ] The most abundant species in a community
    Explanation: A keystone species exerts an influence on its community that is disproportionately large relative to its abundance or biomass. Removing a keystone species causes dramatic changes in community structure. The classic example is the sea star Pisaster in Pacific tide pools — its removal allows mussels to dominate, reducing species diversity from 15+ species to just a few.

  Q5. What is a foundation species?
    [ ] A species whose presence indicates environmental health
    [ ] The first species to colonize a new habitat during succession
    [ ] The species with the largest population in a community
    [*] A species that creates or significantly modifies habitat, enabling many other species to exist in the community
    Explanation: A foundation species creates or significantly modifies the physical habitat, enabling many other species to exist in the community. Unlike keystone species (influence disproportionate to abundance), foundation species typically have high abundance and physically structure the environment. Examples include coral (building reef structure), kelp (creating underwater forests), and large trees (forming forest canopy).

  Q6. What is an indicator species?
    [ ] The first species to arrive in a new habitat
    [ ] A species that always has the largest population
    [*] A species whose presence, absence, or health reflects specific environmental conditions in the community
    [ ] A species that indicates the direction of ecological succession
    Explanation: An indicator species is one whose presence, absence, or health reflects specific environmental conditions in the community. Scientists monitor indicator species to assess ecosystem health. For example, amphibians are indicators of water quality (they absorb pollutants through their skin), and lichens indicate air quality.

  Q7. What is the intermediate disturbance hypothesis?
    [ ] Disturbance has no effect on species diversity in communities
    [*] Species diversity is highest at moderate levels of disturbance because both competitive dominants and early colonizers can coexist
    [ ] Communities with no disturbance have the highest species diversity
    [ ] High disturbance levels always produce the most diverse communities
    Explanation: The intermediate disturbance hypothesis proposes that species diversity is highest at moderate (intermediate) levels of disturbance. Too little disturbance allows competitive dominants to exclude other species; too much disturbance eliminates all but the hardiest colonizers. Moderate disturbance prevents any one species from dominating while not being so frequent that sensitive species cannot establish.

  Q8. What is the difference between a dominant species and a keystone species?
    [ ] They are exactly the same concept with different names
    [*] A dominant species has the greatest abundance or biomass; a keystone species has disproportionate influence relative to its low abundance
    [ ] A dominant species is always a predator while a keystone species is always a plant
    [ ] A dominant species is rare while a keystone species is abundant
    Explanation: A dominant species controls the community through its sheer abundance or biomass (e.g., sugar maples dominating a forest canopy). A keystone species has an effect disproportionate to its relatively low abundance (e.g., sea stars controlling mussel populations). Both influence community structure, but through different mechanisms — abundance vs. outsized ecological role.

  Q9. What is bottom-up control in a community?
    [ ] Middle trophic level organisms control both producers and top predators
    [ ] Predators at the top of the food web control population sizes of lower trophic levels
    [ ] Decomposers control all other organisms in the community
    [*] Community structure is determined by nutrient availability and producer abundance at the base of the food web
    Explanation: Bottom-up control (also called resource-driven control) means community structure is determined by factors at the base of the food web — nutrient availability, sunlight, and producer abundance. More nutrients → more producers → more herbivores → more predators. Changes ripple upward through trophic levels from the bottom.

  Q10. What is top-down control in a community?
    [ ] Producers determine the structure of the entire community
    [ ] The oldest species in a community controls the newest arrivals
    [ ] Sunlight and temperature control all community interactions
    [*] Predators at higher trophic levels regulate the abundance of organisms at lower trophic levels through a trophic cascade
    Explanation: Top-down control (predator-driven control) means that predators at higher trophic levels regulate the abundance of organisms at lower levels. This can create a trophic cascade: removing a top predator causes herbivore populations to explode, which overgrazes producers. The Yellowstone wolf reintroduction is a classic example of restoring top-down control.

  Q11. Which definition BEST describes species richness?
    [ ] The geographic range over which a species is found
    [ ] How evenly individuals are distributed among species in a community
    [ ] The total number of individual organisms living in a community
    [*] The total number of different species present in a community, without regard to their relative abundances
    Explanation: Species richness is the total number of different species present in a community, without considering how many individuals belong to each species. It is the simplest measure of biodiversity — simply a count of species. A coral reef with 1,000 species has greater species richness than a tundra with 50 species.

  Q12. Which definition BEST describes species diversity?
    [ ] The total number of species in a community regardless of abundance
    [ ] The variety of ecosystems across a geographic region
    [*] A measure that combines species richness (number of species) and species evenness (relative abundance of each species) in a community
    [ ] The genetic variation within a single species' population
    Explanation: Species diversity is a composite measure that combines two components: species richness (how many species are present) and species evenness (how equally individuals are distributed among those species). A community with many species that are roughly equal in abundance has higher diversity than one with many species but dominated by just one or two.

  Q13. Which definition BEST describes a keystone species?
    [*] A species that exerts a disproportionately large influence on community structure relative to its abundance or biomass
    [ ] The most abundant or dominant species in a community
    [ ] A species that arrives first during ecological succession
    [ ] A species that physically constructs the habitat for the community
    Explanation: A keystone species is one that exerts a disproportionately large influence on community structure and function relative to its abundance or biomass. The name comes from the keystone of an arch — a small stone that holds the entire structure together. Removing a keystone species causes dramatic changes in community composition. Classic examples include sea otters, wolves, and the sea star Pisaster.

  Q14. Which definition BEST describes an indicator species?
    [*] A species whose presence, absence, or health serves as a measure of environmental conditions or ecosystem health
    [ ] The most visible or noticeable species in a community
    [ ] A species that indicates the direction of prevailing winds in an area
    [ ] A species used to mark territorial boundaries between communities
    Explanation: An indicator species is one whose presence, absence, or population health serves as a measure of environmental conditions or ecosystem health. Scientists monitor these species to detect changes in habitat quality, pollution levels, or climate. Examples include amphibians (sensitive to water quality), lichens (air quality indicators), and trout (clean, cold water indicators).

  Q15. Which definition BEST describes a foundation species?
    [ ] A rare species that has minimal impact on its community
    [ ] The first species to colonize a new habitat during primary succession
    [*] A species that creates or significantly modifies the physical habitat, enabling many other species to live in the community
    [ ] A species at the base of the food chain that provides energy to all other species
    Explanation: A foundation species creates or significantly modifies the physical habitat, enabling many other species to live in the community. Foundation species are typically abundant and physically structure the environment. Examples include corals (building reef frameworks), kelp (creating undersea forests), beavers (creating wetlands with dams), and large trees (forming forest canopy and understory).

  Q16. Which definition BEST describes the intermediate disturbance hypothesis?
    [*] The ecological theory that species diversity is highest at moderate levels of disturbance, because neither competitive dominants nor only hardy colonizers prevail
    [ ] The hypothesis that only intermediate-sized organisms survive disturbances
    [ ] The idea that species diversity increases with increasingly frequent disturbances
    [ ] The theory that all ecological disturbances are harmful to communities
    Explanation: The intermediate disturbance hypothesis states that species diversity is highest at moderate (intermediate) levels of disturbance. With too little disturbance, competitively dominant species exclude others; with too much disturbance, only the hardiest colonizers survive. Moderate disturbance prevents competitive exclusion while allowing sensitive species to persist, maximizing diversity.

  Q17. Which definition BEST describes a dominant species?
    [ ] A species with a disproportionately large effect relative to its small abundance
    [ ] A predator that controls all prey species in the community
    [ ] The largest individual organism in a community
    [*] A species that has the highest abundance or biomass in a community and exerts major control over community structure through sheer numbers
    Explanation: A dominant species is one that has the highest abundance or biomass in a community and exerts major control over community structure through its sheer numbers. Unlike keystone species (disproportionate effect relative to low abundance), dominant species control communities because they are the most prevalent. Examples include sugar maples in deciduous forests and grasses on prairies.

  Q18. Which definition BEST describes bottom-up control?
    [*] A model of community regulation in which nutrient availability and producer abundance at the base of the food web determine the structure of higher trophic levels
    [ ] The idea that organisms at the bottom of the ocean control marine communities
    [ ] A model in which top predators control the abundance of all lower trophic levels
    [ ] A management strategy where lower-level employees make all decisions
    Explanation: Bottom-up control is a model of community regulation in which nutrient availability and producer abundance at the base of the food web determine community structure. Changes ripple upward: more nutrients → more producers → more herbivores → more predators. Ecosystems limited by nutrient availability (like many freshwater lakes) often show strong bottom-up control.

  Q19. Which definition BEST describes top-down control?
    [ ] The idea that the tallest trees in a forest control all other organisms
    [ ] A system where climate is the only factor controlling community structure
    [*] A model of community regulation in which predators at higher trophic levels control the population sizes of organisms at lower trophic levels through a trophic cascade
    [ ] A model in which producers at the base of the food web control all higher levels
    Explanation: Top-down control is a model of community regulation in which predators at higher trophic levels control population sizes at lower levels through trophic cascades. Removing a top predator causes herbivore populations to explode, overgrazing producers. The classic example is otter removal leading to sea urchin explosion and kelp forest destruction.

  Q20. Which definition BEST describes an ecological disturbance?
    [ ] The gradual process of ecological succession after a stable period
    [ ] The normal daily fluctuations in temperature and light in an ecosystem
    [*] An event — natural or human-caused — that disrupts community structure by changing resource availability, the physical environment, or species composition
    [ ] Any interaction between two different species in a community
    Explanation: An ecological disturbance is an event — natural or human-caused — that disrupts community structure by changing resource availability, the physical environment, or species composition. Examples include fires, floods, storms, volcanic eruptions, logging, and disease outbreaks. Disturbances can reset succession and open niches for new species.

  Q21. Robert Paine removed the sea star Pisaster ochraceus from tide pools along the Washington coast in the 1960s. Without the sea star, mussels (Pisaster's preferred prey) quickly dominated the rocks, crowding out barnacles, algae, limpets, and other species. Species diversity dropped from 15 species to 8. When Pisaster was present, it kept mussel populations in check, allowing many other species to coexist. What concept does this experiment BEST demonstrate?
    [ ] Foundation species — Pisaster creates the physical rock habitat
    [ ] Dominant species — Pisaster is the most abundant organism in the tide pool
    [*] Keystone species — Pisaster has a disproportionately large effect on community diversity relative to its abundance
    [ ] Indicator species — Pisaster's health reflects environmental quality
    Explanation: This is the original keystone species experiment. Paine's work showed that Pisaster, despite being relatively uncommon, had a disproportionately large effect on community structure. By preying on competitively dominant mussels, the sea star prevented competitive exclusion and maintained high species diversity. Its removal dramatically reduced diversity — the hallmark of a keystone species.

  Q22. In a tropical rainforest, researchers compare two plots with identical species richness (50 tree species each). In Plot A, each species makes up roughly 2% of all trees. In Plot B, one species comprises 80% of trees and the remaining 49 species share the other 20%. Which plot has higher species diversity, and why?
    [ ] Neither plot can be compared because they are in a tropical rainforest
    [ ] Both plots have identical diversity because they have the same number of species
    [*] Plot A — it has higher species evenness, so its combined diversity (richness + evenness) is greater
    [ ] Plot B — having one dominant species increases diversity
    Explanation: Plot A has higher species diversity because diversity combines both richness AND evenness. Both plots have equal species richness (50 species), but Plot A has much higher evenness (each species equally represented at ~2%). Plot B's heavy dominance by one species (80%) means most species are rare, reducing effective diversity despite equal richness.

  Q23. Coral reefs in the Caribbean support thousands of species by building complex three-dimensional limestone structures. Fish, invertebrates, algae, and countless other organisms live within the reef framework. When coral bleaching kills the reef-building corals, the entire community collapses even though the corals were not the predators or the most numerous individuals. What role do corals play in this community?
    [ ] Dominant species — corals control the reef through predation
    [ ] Indicator species — corals indicate that the water is polluted
    [ ] Keystone species — corals have a disproportionate effect relative to their low abundance
    [*] Foundation species — corals create the physical habitat structure that enables thousands of other species to exist
    Explanation: Corals are foundation species — they physically construct the reef framework (limestone skeleton) that provides the structural habitat for thousands of other species. Unlike keystone species (disproportionate effect relative to low abundance), corals are abundant and their role is specifically habitat creation. When the foundation species is lost, the physical habitat collapses and the entire community it supported disappears.

  Q24. Ecologists studying streams in the Pacific Northwest notice that streams near salmon runs have healthier riparian forests than streams without salmon. Bears and eagles catch salmon and carry them to the forest, where their carcasses decompose and release marine-derived nutrients (nitrogen, phosphorus) that fertilize the trees. The more nutrients available, the more the trees grow, supporting more insects, birds, and mammals. What type of community control does this illustrate?
    [ ] Top-down control — bears and eagles are predators controlling the community from above
    [ ] Intermediate disturbance — salmon runs create moderate disturbance levels
    [ ] Competitive exclusion — salmon outcompete trees for nutrients
    [*] Bottom-up control — nutrient input (from salmon carcasses) at the base of the food web drives productivity at all higher levels
    Explanation: This illustrates bottom-up control — nutrients from decomposing salmon carcasses increase the resource base for producers (trees), which ripples upward through trophic levels: more tree growth → more insects → more birds and mammals. The community structure is driven by nutrient availability at the base. While bears are predators, the community effect described is resource-driven (bottom-up), not predation-driven (top-down).

  Q25. When sea otters were hunted to near extinction along the Pacific coast, sea urchin populations (which otters normally eat) exploded. The unchecked urchins devoured kelp forests, converting lush underwater forests into barren 'urchin barrens' with very few species. After otter populations were protected and recovered, urchins declined, and kelp forests regrew with full biodiversity. What does this BEST illustrate?
    [ ] Indicator species — otters indicate water quality problems
    [ ] Intermediate disturbance — otter hunting was a moderate disturbance
    [ ] Bottom-up control — kelp controls the urchin and otter populations
    [*] Top-down control and trophic cascade — the top predator (otter) regulates herbivores (urchins), which in turn controls producers (kelp)
    Explanation: This is a classic example of top-down control and trophic cascade. Sea otters (top predator) control sea urchin populations (herbivores), which in turn determines kelp abundance (producers). Removing the top predator caused a cascade: otters removed → urchins explode → kelp destroyed → biodiversity collapses. Restoring otters reversed the cascade, demonstrating predator-driven community regulation.

  Q26. A study of grassland communities found that fire frequency determines species diversity. Plots that never burned became dominated by a few tall grass species. Plots burned every year supported only fire-resistant species. But plots burned every 3-5 years had the highest species diversity — both fire-tolerant and fire-sensitive species coexisted. Which concept does this BEST support?
    [ ] Species richness — the plots with fire have more total individual plants
    [*] The intermediate disturbance hypothesis — moderate fire frequency prevents competitive exclusion while allowing sensitive species to persist
    [ ] Top-down control — fire is a predator that controls grass populations
    [ ] Foundation species — fire is a foundation species that builds habitat
    Explanation: This is a textbook example of the intermediate disturbance hypothesis. No fire (low disturbance) → competitive dominants exclude others. Annual fire (high disturbance) → only fire-resistant species survive. Fire every 3-5 years (moderate disturbance) → prevents competitive exclusion but is infrequent enough for fire-sensitive species to establish between burns, maximizing diversity.

  Q27. Scientists monitoring a wetland use frogs as study organisms. They find that declining frog populations correlate with increased pesticide runoff from nearby farms. When the pesticide levels are low, frog populations are healthy and diverse. When they discover that frogs in a new wetland show deformities and declining numbers, they suspect contamination before even testing the water. What ecological role are the frogs serving?
    [*] Indicator species — frog health reflects water quality and environmental conditions in the wetland
    [ ] Foundation species — frogs create the wetland habitat structure
    [ ] Dominant species — frogs are the most abundant organisms in the wetland
    [ ] Keystone species — frogs have a disproportionate community effect
    Explanation: Frogs are serving as indicator species — their presence, health, and population status reflect the environmental conditions of the wetland. Amphibians are excellent indicators because they absorb substances through their permeable skin, live in both water and on land, and are sensitive to pollutants. Scientists use indicator species as early warning systems for environmental problems.

  Q28. In a North American deciduous forest, sugar maples make up 75% of the tree canopy, with oaks, beeches, and other species comprising the rest. The sugar maples shade the forest floor, decompose slowly to create acidic soil, and produce massive amounts of leaf litter. While sugar maples are not irreplaceable in the same way a keystone species would be, their overwhelming abundance shapes the entire forest environment. What ecological role do sugar maples play?
    [ ] Indicator species — sugar maples indicate that the forest soil is acidic
    [*] Dominant species — sugar maples control the community through their high abundance and biomass, shaping environmental conditions
    [ ] Keystone species — sugar maples have a disproportionate effect relative to their low numbers
    [ ] Invasive species — sugar maples outcompete native trees
    Explanation: Sugar maples are a dominant species — they control the community through their overwhelmingly high abundance and biomass (75% of canopy). They shape the forest environment by determining light availability (canopy shading), soil chemistry (acidic leaf litter), and nutrient cycling. This differs from a keystone species, which has disproportionate influence relative to LOW abundance.

  Q29. Lake Victoria in Africa once supported over 500 endemic cichlid fish species — the most species-rich freshwater fish community on Earth. In the 1950s, Nile perch (a large predatory fish) was introduced as a food source. Nile perch ate most of the smaller cichlid species, and eutrophication (nutrient pollution) muddied the water, disrupting visual mating signals that kept cichlid species reproductively isolated. Over 200 cichlid species went extinct. What concepts does this scenario illustrate about community ecology?
    [ ] Bottom-up control — nutrient pollution drove the cichlid extinction
    [ ] The intermediate disturbance hypothesis — moderate introduction of Nile perch increased diversity
    [*] Catastrophic biodiversity loss caused by the combined effects of an invasive predator (Nile perch) and pollution disrupting species interactions
    [ ] Foundation species loss — cichlids built the lake habitat for other species
    Explanation: Lake Victoria illustrates catastrophic species diversity loss from multiple human impacts acting together. The Nile perch (invasive species) directly consumed cichlids, while eutrophication (pollution) muddied the water and disrupted the visual mate recognition signals that maintained reproductive isolation between similar cichlid species. This combination destroyed the highest species richness of any freshwater fish community — showing how multiple threats compound to devastate biodiversity.

  Q30. Scientists compare two neighboring marine communities over 10 years. Community A, a seagrass bed, has 150 species and high evenness (many species in balanced proportions) and recovers quickly after a hurricane. Community B, an area with one dominant invasive algae covering 90% of the substrate and 40 other sparse species, takes years to recover after the same hurricane and loses 15 species permanently. What principle about biodiversity and community resilience does this comparison BEST illustrate?
    [*] Higher species diversity (richness + evenness) makes communities more resilient — able to resist and recover from disturbance more effectively
    [ ] Hurricanes are beneficial because they remove invasive species
    [ ] Low-diversity communities are more resilient because they have simpler food webs
    [ ] Community resilience depends only on species richness, not evenness
    Explanation: This comparison illustrates the diversity-stability hypothesis — communities with higher species diversity (both richness AND evenness) are more resilient against disturbance. Community A's high diversity provides functional redundancy (if one species is lost, others can fill its role) and balanced interactions, enabling rapid recovery. Community B's low evenness (dominated by one species) makes it fragile — if the dominant species is damaged, few alternatives exist to maintain ecosystem function.
